First Condition: al-Ittirād (Coextensiveness)
First condition of definition: coextensiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition satisfies the condition of ittirād and provide an example of a violation.

Core Idea
A good definition must prevent non-instances from entering.

The first condition for a correct definition is that it must be coextensive (ittirād). This means the definition should only apply to the definiendum and not to anything else. We'll explore what this means and why defining 'man' as 'animal' fails this condition. We also note the purpose of definition: sufficient conceptual knowledge to build assertive knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the purpose of definition
The author states that the purpose of definition is to provide sufficient conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) to build assertive knowledge (tasdiq). This frames why conditions are needed: the definition must be epistemically useful.
This purpose is common to all five conditions.
2
Identify the condition of ittirād
Ittirād means that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies. The definition acts as a 'man'' (preventer) against including non-definienda.
The Arabic term 'ittirād' is derived from 'ṭarada' meaning to drive away or repel.
3
Apply to an example
Defining 'man' as 'animal' is invalid because it includes non-human animals. The definition is not coextensive, so the condition is violated.
This example is given in the text and clearly illustrates the failure.
4
Test a definition yourself
Consider the definition of 'salah' as 'specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Does it satisfy ittirād? (Hint: Does it include acts not considered salah?)
This will be practiced in 'apply_the_tool'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شروط التعريف: • يشترط للحد والرسم شروط لكي تكون تعريفات صحيحة، فإن المقصود من التعريف المعرفة الكافية لبناء المطلوب عليها، وهذه الشروط هي: :أولاً: الاطّراد؛ يعني أن يكون الحد مُطَرداً، ومفهوم الاطراد هو كلما وُجِدَ التعريف وجد المعرَّف فيكون مانعاً من دخول أفراد غير المعرَّف فيه. فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بالحيوان فقط لدخول غيره فيه، فليس بمانع. (4) أى المعرفة التصورية الكافية لبناء المطلوب التصديقى عليها. لأن التصور شرط أو شطر التصديق فالتصديق مبنى على التصوّر. (5) أى فى الذهن. والمعنى أنه كلما ذكر التعريف أى حضر عند العقل، استدعى العقل صورةً عقلية للمعرف. ولا تفهم من قولنا صورة أنها صورة حسية، فليس ذلك شرطاً في هذه الصورة. ثانياً: الانعكاس ؛ يعني كلما وُجِدَ المعرَّف وُجد التعريف، فيكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف لا يخرج عنه منها شيء (1) فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بـ الحيوان الكاتب بالفعل ] الخروج أفراده من غير الكاتب عن هذا التعريف، فليس بجامع. ثالثاً: أن يكون التعريف أظهر من المعرَّف ، وأوضح منه، لا أخفى، فلا يصح تعريف النار مثلاً بأنها جسم كالنَّفس، لأن النفس أخفى من النار. وكذلك لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مساوياً للمعرف، لأن المساوي في الظهور والخفاء ليس بأوضح ، فلا يقال مثلاً في تعريف المتحرك هو ما ليس بساكن. رابعاً: لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مشتملاً على كلمة مجازية، إلا إن كانت تقترن معها قرينة بينة واضحة، فالقرينة تفيد في بيان المعنى المقصود حتى وإن كان مجازاً بالنسبة لأصل استعمال الكلمة، فالمجاز مع القرينة حقيقة في محلها. (2) فلو عرفنا العالم بقولنا مثلاً هو بحر يدخل الحمام، أو بحر يصلي ويصوم، فهذا لا يصح لأنه لا يحتوي على قرينة بيّنة تظهر المعنى، فالمعنى ملتبس ، فإن كان مع المجاز قرينة تبينه وتوضحه جاز كأن يقال البليد: حيوان ناهق يدخل الحمام. (3) خامساً: لا يجوز أن يدخل في التعريف ما لا يعرف إلا بنفس التعريف فهذا يؤدي إلى الدور، فيمتنع. ومثاله تعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم، مع أن المعلوم تتوقف معرفته على معرفة العلم لاشتقاقه منه. ويمكن أن يجاب ببعض الأجوبة عن ذلك ، ولكنها غير متبادرة إلى الذهن، وغير ظاهرة، والتعريف يجب أن يكون أوضح وأظهر من المعرف. (4) وتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم هو تعريف لسان الأمة القاضي الباقلاني لا زالت سحائب الرحمة على روحه تتوالى. والجواب الأليق عن سؤال الدور، هو أن المراد بالمعلوم ذاته، لا باعتبار كونه معلوماً. فكأنه قيل
Conditions of definition: Conditions are required for the hadd and rasm so that definitions are correct. For the purpose of definition is sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired (tasdiq) upon it. These conditions are: First: al-ittitrad; meaning that the definition should be coextensive, and the concept of ittirād is that whenever the definition exists, the definiendum exists, so it prevents the entry of individuals other than the definiendum into it. So it is not permissible to define man by 'animal' alone because others enter into it, so it is not a preventer. (4) i.e., sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired assertive knowledge upon it. Because conception is a condition or part of assent, so assent is built upon conception. (5) i.e., in the mind. And the meaning is that whenever the definition is mentioned, i.e., present in the mind, the mind calls to mind a mental image of the defined. And do not understand from our saying 'image' that it is a sensory image, for that is not a condition for this image. Second: al-in'ikās; meaning that whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists, so it includes all individuals of the defined, not excluding any of them (1). So it is not permissible to define man as 'actually writing animal' because individuals who are not writers would be excluded from this definition, so it is not inclusive. Third: The definition should be more apparent (azhar) than the definiendum, and clearer, not hidden. So it is not valid to define fire, for example, as 'a body like the soul', because the soul is more hidden than fire. Likewise, it is not valid for the definition to be equal in clarity to the definiendum, because equality in clarity is not clearer. So one should not say, for example, in defining 'moving' it is 'what is not stationary'. Fourth: It is not valid for the definition to include a metaphorical word, unless it is accompanied by a clear and evident qarina (indicator). For the qarina helps in clarifying the intended meaning even if it is metaphorical in relation to the original use of the word. So metaphor with a qarina is in effect a real meaning in its context. (2) So if we define 'alim (scholar) by our saying, for example, 'he is a sea that enters the bathhouse', or 'a sea that prays and fasts', this is not valid because it does not contain a clear qarina that shows the meaning, so the meaning is ambiguous. But if there is with the metaphor a qarina that explains and clarifies it, it is permissible, as if one says about the dullard: 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse'. (3) Fifth: It is not permissible to include in the definition something that is not known except through the definition itself, for this leads to circularity (dawr), so it is prohibited. An example is defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known', while the known's knowledge depends on knowledge of 'ilm due to its derivation from it. And it is possible to answer with some answers about that, but they are not readily apparent, and the definition must be clearer and more apparent than the definiendum. (4) And the definition of knowledge as 'ma'rifa of the known' is the definition of Lisan al-Ummah al-Qadi al-Baqillani – may the clouds of mercy continue to pour upon his soul. And the most appropriate answer to the question of circularity is that by 'the known' is meant its essence, not insofar as it is known. It is as if it were said...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents five conditions that any valid definition (hadd or rasm) must satisfy. The author first states the purpose of definition: sufficient conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) to build assertive knowledge (tasdiq). The conditions are enumerated:

First: al-ittirād (consistency/coextensiveness). The definition must be 'manifold' such that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies. This makes the definition a man' (preventer) against including non-defined individuals. Example: defining man as 'animal' is invalid because it includes non-human animals.

Second: al-in'ikās (converse). Whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists. This makes the definition inclusive of all individuals of the definiendum. Example: defining man as 'actually writing animal' excludes non-writers, so it is not inclusive.

Third: The definition must be more apparent and clearer than the definiendum. It cannot be more obscure, nor equal in clarity. Example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is less known. Similarly, defining 'moving' as 'what is not stationary' is circular in a way (equality of clarity).

Fourth: The definition should not contain a metaphorical term without a clear qarina (contextual indicator). If a clear qarina is present, the metaphor is treated as a real definition in that context. Example of invalid: 'a scholar is a sea that enters the bathhouse' – ambiguous. Valid example: 'the dullard is a braying animal that enters the bathhouse' where the qarina 'braying animal' clarifies the metaphorical meaning.

Fifth: The definition must not be circular (dawr). That is, the definiens should not depend on the definiendum. Example given: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known' is circular because 'known' derives from 'ilm. The author notes that al-Qadi al-Baqillani gave this definition, and the best answer to the circularity objection is to interpret 'the known' as the essence itself, not as known.

Footnotes (4) and (5) explain the purpose and mental aspect. Footnotes (1)-(4) on page 44 elaborate on the conditions with additional examples and clarifications, including the discussion about al-Baqillani.

Key Terms
اطراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following definitions of 'man' violates the condition of ittirād?
A) Man is a rational animal
B) Man is an animal
C) Man is a featherless biped
D) Man is a writing animal

Key Takeaway
Ittirād ensures that a definition is not too broad by requiring that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check whether the definition of 'salah' as 'specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' satisfies ittirād. Does it include acts not considered salah? Explain your reasoning.
?
Provide an example of a definition from fiqh that you think might violate ittirād and explain why.

Hadd and Rasm
beginner
5 min
Second Condition: al-In'ikās (Converse)
Second condition of definition: converse/inclusiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition satisfies the condition of in'ikās and provide an example of a violation.

Core Idea
A good definition must include all instances of the definiendum.

The second condition for a correct definition is that it must be converse (in'ikās): whenever the definiendum exists, the definition must also apply. This ensures the definition is not too narrow. We'll see why defining 'man' as 'actually writing animal' fails this condition.

Guided Path
1
Recall the meaning of in'ikās
In'ikās means that whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists. The definition must be inclusive (jāmi') of all individuals of the definiendum.
This is the opposite direction from ittirād.
2
Identify the violation in the example
Defining 'man' as 'an animal that actually writes' excludes non-writers, so not all humans are included. Thus it fails in'ikās.
The text gives this example: 'al-ḥayawān al-kātib bi-al-fi'l'.
3
Contrast with a valid definition
A valid definition must be both coextensive (ittirād) and inclusive (in'ikās). For example, 'man is a rational animal' satisfies both: it includes all humans and excludes non-humans.
Rational animal is a classic example of a complete definition (hadd).

Third Condition: Clarity (Azhar)
Third condition: definition must be clearer than definiendum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a definition is clearer (azhar) than the definiendum and identify violations.

Core Idea
A definition must be more apparent and clearer than what it defines.

The third condition requires that the definition be more apparent and clearer (azhar) than the definiendum. It cannot be more obscure or even equally clear. We'll see why defining fire as 'a body like the soul' fails, and why defining 'moving' as 'what is not stationary' is also invalid.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of clarity
The definition must be more apparent (azhar) and clearer (awḍaḥ) than the definiendum. It cannot be more hidden or equally clear.
This ensures the definition fulfills its purpose of providing knowledge.
2
Analyze the example of fire
Defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is less known than fire. The soul's nature is obscure, so the definition does not clarify.
This example shows the violation of using a more obscure term.
3
Analyze the example of moving
Defining 'moving' (mutaharrik) as 'what is not stationary' (mā laysa bi-sākin) is invalid because the definition is equal in clarity to the definiendum – it is simply a negation.
The definition must be a positive clarification, not a mere negation of the opposite.
4
General principle
The definition should introduce concepts that are better known or more fundamental than the definiendum.
If the definiens is equally or less known, the definition fails.

شروط التعريف: • يشترط للحد والرسم شروط لكي تكون تعريفات صحيحة، فإن المقصود من التعريف المعرفة الكافية لبناء المطلوب عليها، وهذه الشروط هي: :أولاً: الاطّراد؛ يعني أن يكون الحد مُطَرداً، ومفهوم الاطراد هو كلما وُجِدَ التعريف وجد المعرَّف فيكون مانعاً من دخول أفراد غير المعرَّف فيه. فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بالحيوان فقط لدخول غيره فيه، فليس بمانع. (4) أى المعرفة التصورية الكافية لبناء المطلوب التصديقى عليها. لأن التصور شرط أو شطر التصديق فالتصديق مبنى على التصوّر. (5) أى فى الذهن. والمعنى أنه كلما ذكر التعريف أى حضر عند العقل، استدعى العقل صورةً عقلية للمعرف. ولا تفهم من قولنا صورة أنها صورة حسية، فليس ذلك شرطاً في هذه الصورة. ثانياً: الانعكاس ؛ يعني كلما وُجِدَ المعرَّف وُجد التعريف، فيكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف لا يخرج عنه منها شيء (1) فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بـ الحيوان الكاتب بالفعل ] الخروج أفراده من غير الكاتب عن هذا التعريف، فليس بجامع. ثالثاً: أن يكون التعريف أظهر من المعرَّف ، وأوضح منه، لا أخفى، فلا يصح تعريف النار مثلاً بأنها جسم كالنَّفس، لأن النفس أخفى من النار. وكذلك لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مساوياً للمعرف، لأن المساوي في الظهور والخفاء ليس بأوضح ، فلا يقال مثلاً في تعريف المتحرك هو ما ليس بساكن. رابعاً: لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مشتملاً على كلمة مجازية، إلا إن كانت تقترن معها قرينة بينة واضحة، فالقرينة تفيد في بيان المعنى المقصود حتى وإن كان مجازاً بالنسبة لأصل استعمال الكلمة، فالمجاز مع القرينة حقيقة في محلها. (2) فلو عرفنا العالم بقولنا مثلاً هو بحر يدخل الحمام، أو بحر يصلي ويصوم، فهذا لا يصح لأنه لا يحتوي على قرينة بيّنة تظهر المعنى، فالمعنى ملتبس ، فإن كان مع المجاز قرينة تبينه وتوضحه جاز كأن يقال البليد: حيوان ناهق يدخل الحمام. (3) خامساً: لا يجوز أن يدخل في التعريف ما لا يعرف إلا بنفس التعريف فهذا يؤدي إلى الدور، فيمتنع. ومثاله تعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم، مع أن المعلوم تتوقف معرفته على معرفة العلم لاشتقاقه منه. ويمكن أن يجاب ببعض الأجوبة عن ذلك ، ولكنها غير متبادرة إلى الذهن، وغير ظاهرة، والتعريف يجب أن يكون أوضح وأظهر من المعرف. (4) وتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم هو تعريف لسان الأمة القاضي الباقلاني لا زالت سحائب الرحمة على روحه تتوالى. والجواب الأليق عن سؤال الدور، هو أن المراد بالمعلوم ذاته، لا باعتبار كونه معلوماً. فكأنه قيل
Conditions of definition: Conditions are required for the hadd and rasm so that definitions are correct. For the purpose of definition is sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired (tasdiq) upon it. These conditions are: First: al-ittitrad; meaning that the definition should be coextensive, and the concept of ittirād is that whenever the definition exists, the definiendum exists, so it prevents the entry of individuals other than the definiendum into it. So it is not permissible to define man by 'animal' alone because others enter into it, so it is not a preventer. (4) i.e., sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired assertive knowledge upon it. Because conception is a condition or part of assent, so assent is built upon conception. (5) i.e., in the mind. And the meaning is that whenever the definition is mentioned, i.e., present in the mind, the mind calls to mind a mental image of the defined. And do not understand from our saying 'image' that it is a sensory image, for that is not a condition for this image. Second: al-in'ikās; meaning that whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists, so it includes all individuals of the defined, not excluding any of them (1). So it is not permissible to define man as 'actually writing animal' because individuals who are not writers would be excluded from this definition, so it is not inclusive. Third: The definition should be more apparent (azhar) than the definiendum, and clearer, not hidden. So it is not valid to define fire, for example, as 'a body like the soul', because the soul is more hidden than fire. Likewise, it is not valid for the definition to be equal in clarity to the definiendum, because equality in clarity is not clearer. So one should not say, for example, in defining 'moving' it is 'what is not stationary'. Fourth: It is not valid for the definition to include a metaphorical word, unless it is accompanied by a clear and evident qarina (indicator). For the qarina helps in clarifying the intended meaning even if it is metaphorical in relation to the original use of the word. So metaphor with a qarina is in effect a real meaning in its context. (2) So if we define 'alim (scholar) by our saying, for example, 'he is a sea that enters the bathhouse', or 'a sea that prays and fasts', this is not valid because it does not contain a clear qarina that shows the meaning, so the meaning is ambiguous. But if there is with the metaphor a qarina that explains and clarifies it, it is permissible, as if one says about the dullard: 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse'. (3) Fifth: It is not permissible to include in the definition something that is not known except through the definition itself, for this leads to circularity (dawr), so it is prohibited. An example is defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known', while the known's knowledge depends on knowledge of 'ilm due to its derivation from it. And it is possible to answer with some answers about that, but they are not readily apparent, and the definition must be clearer and more apparent than the definiendum. (4) And the definition of knowledge as 'ma'rifa of the known' is the definition of Lisan al-Ummah al-Qadi al-Baqillani – may the clouds of mercy continue to pour upon his soul. And the most appropriate answer to the question of circularity is that by 'the known' is meant its essence, not insofar as it is known. It is as if it were said...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents five conditions that any valid definition (hadd or rasm) must satisfy. The author first states the purpose of definition: sufficient conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) to build assertive knowledge (tasdiq). The conditions are enumerated:

First: al-ittirād (consistency/coextensiveness). The definition must be 'manifold' such that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies. This makes the definition a man' (preventer) against including non-defined individuals. Example: defining man as 'animal' is invalid because it includes non-human animals.

Second: al-in'ikās (converse). Whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists. This makes the definition inclusive of all individuals of the definiendum. Example: defining man as 'actually writing animal' excludes non-writers, so it is not inclusive.

Third: The definition must be more apparent and clearer than the definiendum. It cannot be more obscure, nor equal in clarity. Example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is less known. Similarly, defining 'moving' as 'what is not stationary' is circular in a way (equality of clarity).

Fourth: The definition should not contain a metaphorical term without a clear qarina (contextual indicator). If a clear qarina is present, the metaphor is treated as a real definition in that context. Example of invalid: 'a scholar is a sea that enters the bathhouse' – ambiguous. Valid example: 'the dullard is a braying animal that enters the bathhouse' where the qarina 'braying animal' clarifies the metaphorical meaning.

Fifth: The definition must not be circular (dawr). That is, the definiens should not depend on the definiendum. Example given: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known' is circular because 'known' derives from 'ilm. The author notes that al-Qadi al-Baqillani gave this definition, and the best answer to the circularity objection is to interpret 'the known' as the essence itself, not as known.

Footnotes (4) and (5) explain the purpose and mental aspect. Footnotes (1)-(4) on page 44 elaborate on the conditions with additional examples and clarifications, including the discussion about al-Baqillani.

Key Terms
أظهر
أوضح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is defining fire as 'a body like the soul' invalid?
A) It includes non-fire entities
B) It excludes some fires
C) The soul is more obscure than fire
D) It contains a metaphor

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be epistemically prior: it must be clearer and more apparent than the definiendum.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the following definition of 'istidlāl' (deduction): 'Istidlāl is what is not a direct proof.' Does it satisfy the clarity condition? Explain.
?
Find a definition from Islamic sciences (e.g., from usul al-fiqh) that you think might violate the clarity condition and suggest a clearer alternative.

Fourth Condition: No Metaphor without Clear Qarina
Fourth condition: metaphorical definitions require clear qarina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess whether a metaphorical definition is valid by checking for a clear qarina.

Core Idea
Metaphorical definitions are only valid with an explicit contextual indicator.

The fourth condition addresses the use of metaphorical language in definitions. Metaphor is allowed only if accompanied by a clear qarina (contextual indicator) that clarifies the intended meaning. We'll see examples of invalid and valid metaphorical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule on metaphor
A definition should not contain a metaphorical word unless a clear qarina is present. The qarina makes the metaphor function like a literal expression in that context.
Qarina can be verbal or contextual.
2
Analyze the invalid example
Defining 'alim (scholar) as 'a sea that enters the bathhouse' is invalid because there is no clear qarina to indicate what 'sea' means here. It is ambiguous.
The example uses 'bahr' (sea) metaphorically for vast knowledge, but without qarina.
3
Analyze the valid example
Defining the dullard (al-balīd) as 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse' is valid because 'braying animal' (ḥayawān nāhiq) acts as a qarina, clarifying that the creature is a donkey, so 'entering bathhouse' is metaphorical for dullness.
The qarina 'braying animal' restricts the meaning.
4
Generalize the principle
If a definition uses figurative speech, it must be accompanied by something that points to the intended meaning. Otherwise, it is rejected.
This ensures definitions are unambiguous.

Fifth Condition: No Circularity (Dawr)
Fifth condition: prohibition of circularity in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions and evaluate proposed solutions to circularity.

Core Idea
A definition must not be circular, where the definiendum depends on the definiens and vice versa.

The fifth condition prohibits circularity (dawr) in definitions. The definition must not rely on the definiendum in a way that makes it uninformative. We examine the classic example of defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known' and discuss how al-Qadi al-Baqillani's definition can be defended.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of circularity
Circularity (dawr) occurs when the definition (definiens) can only be known by first knowing the definiendum. This makes the definition useless.
The definition should be epistemically prior.
2
Analyze the example
Defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known' is circular because 'known' (ma'lūm) derives from 'ilm. To know 'ma'lūm', you need to know 'ilm, so the definition presupposes the definiendum.
This example is attributed to al-Qadi al-Baqillani.
3
Examine the proposed resolution
The text mentions that the best reply is to interpret 'the known' as the essence (dhāt) not insofar as it is known. That is, 'ma'lūm' here refers to the thing itself, not its property of being known. This avoids circularity.
This resolution is not immediately apparent, which is why the definition is still problematic.
4
Generalize the principle
Any definition where the definiens implicitly contains the definiendum is invalid due to circularity.
The definition must be independent and clearer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(5) أى فى الذهن. والمعنى أنه كلما ذكر التعريف أى حضر عند العقل، استدعى العقل صورةً عقلية للمعرف. ولا تفهم من قولنا صورة أنها صورة حسية، فليس ذلك شرطاً في هذه الصورة. ثانياً: الانعكاس ؛ يعني كلما وُجِدَ المعرَّف وُجد التعريف، فيكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف لا يخرج عنه منها شيء (1) فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بـ الحيوان الكاتب بالفعل ] الخروج أفراده من غير الكاتب عن هذا التعريف، فليس بجامع. ثالثاً: أن يكون التعريف أظهر من المعرَّف ، وأوضح منه، لا أخفى، فلا يصح تعريف النار مثلاً بأنها جسم كالنَّفس، لأن النفس أخفى من النار. وكذلك لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مساوياً للمعرف، لأن المساوي في الظهور والخفاء ليس بأوضح ، فلا يقال مثلاً في تعريف المتحرك هو ما ليس بساكن. رابعاً: لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مشتملاً على كلمة مجازية، إلا إن كانت تقترن معها قرينة بينة واضحة، فالقرينة تفيد في بيان المعنى المقصود حتى وإن كان مجازاً بالنسبة لأصل استعمال الكلمة، فالمجاز مع القرينة حقيقة في محلها. (2) فلو عرفنا العالم بقولنا مثلاً هو بحر يدخل الحمام، أو بحر يصلي ويصوم، فهذا لا يصح لأنه لا يحتوي على قرينة بيّنة تظهر المعنى، فالمعنى ملتبس ، فإن كان مع المجاز قرينة تبينه وتوضحه جاز كأن يقال البليد: حيوان ناهق يدخل الحمام. (3) خامساً: لا يجوز أن يدخل في التعريف ما لا يعرف إلا بنفس التعريف فهذا يؤدي إلى الدور، فيمتنع. ومثاله تعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم، مع أن المعلوم تتوقف معرفته على معرفة العلم لاشتقاقه منه. ويمكن أن يجاب ببعض الأجوبة عن ذلك ، ولكنها غير متبادرة إلى الذهن، وغير ظاهرة، والتعريف يجب أن يكون أوضح وأظهر من المعرف. (4) وتعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم هو تعريف لسان الأمة القاضي الباقلاني لا زالت سحائب الرحمة على روحه تتوالى. والجواب الأليق عن سؤال الدور، هو أن المراد بالمعلوم ذاته، لا باعتبار كونه معلوماً. فكأنه قيل
Conditions of definition: Conditions are required for the hadd and rasm so that definitions are correct. For the purpose of definition is sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired (tasdiq) upon it. These conditions are: First: al-ittitrad; meaning that the definition should be coextensive, and the concept of ittirād is that whenever the definition exists, the definiendum exists, so it prevents the entry of individuals other than the definiendum into it. So it is not permissible to define man by 'animal' alone because others enter into it, so it is not a preventer. (4) i.e., sufficient conceptual knowledge to build the desired assertive knowledge upon it. Because conception is a condition or part of assent, so assent is built upon conception. (5) i.e., in the mind. And the meaning is that whenever the definition is mentioned, i.e., present in the mind, the mind calls to mind a mental image of the defined. And do not understand from our saying 'image' that it is a sensory image, for that is not a condition for this image. Second: al-in'ikās; meaning that whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists, so it includes all individuals of the defined, not excluding any of them (1). So it is not permissible to define man as 'actually writing animal' because individuals who are not writers would be excluded from this definition, so it is not inclusive. Third: The definition should be more apparent (azhar) than the definiendum, and clearer, not hidden. So it is not valid to define fire, for example, as 'a body like the soul', because the soul is more hidden than fire. Likewise, it is not valid for the definition to be equal in clarity to the definiendum, because equality in clarity is not clearer. So one should not say, for example, in defining 'moving' it is 'what is not stationary'. Fourth: It is not valid for the definition to include a metaphorical word, unless it is accompanied by a clear and evident qarina (indicator). For the qarina helps in clarifying the intended meaning even if it is metaphorical in relation to the original use of the word. So metaphor with a qarina is in effect a real meaning in its context. (2) So if we define 'alim (scholar) by our saying, for example, 'he is a sea that enters the bathhouse', or 'a sea that prays and fasts', this is not valid because it does not contain a clear qarina that shows the meaning, so the meaning is ambiguous. But if there is with the metaphor a qarina that explains and clarifies it, it is permissible, as if one says about the dullard: 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse'. (3) Fifth: It is not permissible to include in the definition something that is not known except through the definition itself, for this leads to circularity (dawr), so it is prohibited. An example is defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known', while the known's knowledge depends on knowledge of 'ilm due to its derivation from it. And it is possible to answer with some answers about that, but they are not readily apparent, and the definition must be clearer and more apparent than the definiendum. (4) And the definition of knowledge as 'ma'rifa of the known' is the definition of Lisan al-Ummah al-Qadi al-Baqillani – may the clouds of mercy continue to pour upon his soul. And the most appropriate answer to the question of circularity is that by 'the known' is meant its essence, not insofar as it is known. It is as if it were said...
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents five conditions that any valid definition (hadd or rasm) must satisfy. The author first states the purpose of definition: sufficient conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) to build assertive knowledge (tasdiq). The conditions are enumerated:

First: al-ittirād (consistency/coextensiveness). The definition must be 'manifold' such that whenever the definition applies, the definiendum applies. This makes the definition a man' (preventer) against including non-defined individuals. Example: defining man as 'animal' is invalid because it includes non-human animals.

Second: al-in'ikās (converse). Whenever the definiendum exists, the definition exists. This makes the definition inclusive of all individuals of the definiendum. Example: defining man as 'actually writing animal' excludes non-writers, so it is not inclusive.

Third: The definition must be more apparent and clearer than the definiendum. It cannot be more obscure, nor equal in clarity. Example: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is less known. Similarly, defining 'moving' as 'what is not stationary' is circular in a way (equality of clarity).

Fourth: The definition should not contain a metaphorical term without a clear qarina (contextual indicator). If a clear qarina is present, the metaphor is treated as a real definition in that context. Example of invalid: 'a scholar is a sea that enters the bathhouse' – ambiguous. Valid example: 'the dullard is a braying animal that enters the bathhouse' where the qarina 'braying animal' clarifies the metaphorical meaning.

Fifth: The definition must not be circular (dawr). That is, the definiens should not depend on the definiendum. Example given: defining 'ilm (knowledge) as 'ma'rifa of the known' is circular because 'known' derives from 'ilm. The author notes that al-Qadi al-Baqillani gave this definition, and the best answer to the circularity objection is to interpret 'the known' as the essence itself, not as known.

Footnotes (4) and (5) explain the purpose and mental aspect. Footnotes (1)-(4) on page 44 elaborate on the conditions with additional examples and clarifications, including the discussion about al-Baqillani.

Key Terms
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'ma'rifa' is itself knowledge
B) Because 'the known' derives from 'ilm, so it presupposes knowledge
C) Because it includes a metaphor
D) Because it is not coextensive

Key Takeaway
Circularity (dawr) invalidates a definition because the definiens depends on the definiendum, defeating the purpose of clarification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify a potential circular definition from Islamic sciences (e.g., defining 'wajib' as 'what the Lawgiver demands' – is 'demand' dependent on 'wajib'? Discuss.

Condition of Convertibility (al-Intikās)
States the second condition for a valid definition: convertibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given definition satisfies the condition of convertibility (al-intikās) by checking for comprehensiveness (jāmi').

Core Idea
A valid definition must cover every instance of the thing it defines.

You learned that a definition must be comprehensive, meaning it includes every individual of the definiendum. This is the condition of convertibility (al-intikās). In this lesson, we apply this condition to test definitions commonly used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition
Locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the phrase used to define it. In the example, 'human' (al-insān) is defined as 'actually writing animal' (al-ḥayawān al-kātib bi al-fi'l).
2
List the individuals of the definiendum
Consider all individuals that fall under the definiendum. For 'human', this includes every human being, whether writing or not, old or young, etc.
3
Check if the definition applies to all individuals
Examine if the definition covers each individual. The definition 'actually writing animal' excludes non-writers (e.g., infants, illiterates), so it is not comprehensive.
If any individual is excluded, the definition fails the convertibility condition.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Practice with definitions from Islamic law. For instance, defining 'ṣalāh' as 'specific actions and words beginning with takbīr and ending with taslīm' – this covers all valid prayers, so it satisfies convertibility.
If a definition leaves out a genuine instance, it is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً: الانعكاس؛ يعني كلما وجد المعرَّف وجد التعريف، فيكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف لا يخرج عنه منها، فلا يجوز تعريف الإنسان بالحيوان الكاتب بالفعل، لأن غير الكاتب يخرج عن هذا التعريف، فليس بجامع. (1) أى لا يخرج عن التعريف من أفراد المعرف شيء.
Second: Convertibility; meaning whenever the definiendum is found, the definition is found, so it is comprehensive for the individuals of the definiendum, not excluding any of them. So it is not permissible to define 'human' as 'actually writing animal', because non-writers exit from this definition, so it is not comprehensive. (1) That is, no individual of the definiendum is excluded from the definition.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage explains the second condition for a valid definition, called al-intikās (convertibility). The condition requires that whenever the definiendum (the thing being defined) exists, the definition must apply to it. This means the definition must be jāmi' (comprehensive), covering all individuals of the definiendum. The author gives an example: if we define 'human' as 'actually writing animal', this definition would exclude non-writers, so it is not comprehensive and thus invalid. The footnote clarifies that no individual of the definiendum should be excluded from the definition. This condition ensures the definition is coextensive with the definiendum.

The Definition Must Be Clearer Than the Definiendum
States the third condition: the definition must be clearer than the definiendum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given definition satisfies the condition of being clearer than the definiendum.

Core Idea
A definition must illuminate, not obscure — it must be more apparent than what it defines.

A good definition does not just replace one unknown with another; it must actually increase clarity. This lesson explores the third condition from the Sullam al-Munawraq: the definition must be aẓhar (more apparent) and awḍaḥ (more obvious) than the definiendum. We will see why defining fire as 'a body like the soul' fails, and why 'moving is not stationary' is equally unhelpful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition
In any definition, first locate the term being defined (definiendum) and the proposed definition. For example, in 'fire is a body like the soul', fire is the definiendum, and 'a body like the soul' is the definition.
2
Assess the relative clarity
Compare how well-known each term is. The definition must be more obvious than the definiendum. If the definition uses a term that is more obscure (like 'soul' compared to 'fire'), the condition is violated.
Clarity here refers to how easily the term is known, not its logical precision.
3
Check for equality in clarity
Even if the definition is not more obscure, it must be more apparent. If it is equally clear (musāwin), it fails because it adds no illumination. For instance, defining 'moving' as 'not stationary' merely swaps synonyms without clarifying.
This condition prevents circular or tautological definitions.
4
Apply the condition to other examples
Test definitions from Islamic sciences. For example, in fiqh, defining 'wine' as 'intoxicating grape juice' is valid because 'intoxicating' clarifies the ruling; but defining 'intoxicant' as 'that which causes drunkenness' might be equally unclear.
Think of definitions in usul al-fiqh or kalam that you have encountered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً: أن يكون التعريف أظهر من المعرَّف، وأوضح منه، لا أخفى، فلا يصح تعريف النار مثلاً بأنها جسم كالنفس، لأن النفس أخفى من النار. وكذلك لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مساوياً للمعرف، لأن المساوي في الظهور والخفاء ليس بأوضح، فلا يقال مثلاً في تعريف المتحرك هو ما ليس بساكن.
Third: The definition should be more apparent than the definiendum, and more obvious, not more obscure. So it is not valid to define fire, for example, as a body like the soul, because the soul is more obscure than fire. Likewise, it is not valid for the definition to be equal to the definiendum, because that which is equal in clarity and obscurity is not more obvious. So one should not say, for example, in defining the moving that it is that which is not stationary.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the third condition: the definition must be aẓhar (more apparent) and awḍaḥ (more obvious) than the definiendum. The author gives two examples: defining fire as 'a body like the soul' is invalid because the soul is less known than fire. Also, defining a moving thing as 'not stationary' is invalid because it is equally clear, not more obvious. The definition must clarify, not obscure.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must be more apparent and obvious than the term it defines, not equal or more obscure.

Condition Four: Avoid Metaphorical Terms Without Clear Indicator
States the fourth condition: definition must not contain metaphorical terms without a clear indicator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition improperly uses a metaphorical term without a clear contextual clue.

Core Idea
A metaphor in a definition is only valid if a clear clue (qarīna) makes it unambiguous.

In defining terms, clarity is paramount. The fourth condition in al-Sullam warns against using metaphorical language without a clear indicator. This lesson will teach you to spot problematic metaphors and apply the rule correctly using Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage (e.g., 'al-ʿālim' or 'al-balīd').
2
Check for metaphorical term
Determine if any word in the definition is used non-literally (e.g., 'sea' for a scholar).
3
Locate the qarīna
Look for a contextual clue that clarifies the intended meaning (e.g., 'braying' disambiguates the animal).
4
Assess clarity
Does the qarīna remove all ambiguity? If not, the definition is invalid.
5
Apply to new examples
Use the same steps to evaluate other definitions, such as those from fiqh or usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً: لا يصح أن يكون التعريف مشتملاً على كلمة مجازية، إلا إن كانت تقترن معها قرينة بينة واضحة، فالقرينة تفيد في بيان المعنى المقصود حتى وإن كان مجازاً بالنسبة لأصل استعمال الكلمة، فالمجاز مع القرينة حقيقة في محلها. فلو عرفنا العالم بقولنا مثلاً هو بحر يدخل الحمام، أو بحر يصلي ويصوم، فهذا لا يصح لأنه لا يحتوي على قرينة بيّنة تظهر المعنى، فالمعنى ملتبس، فإن كان مع المجاز قرينة تبينه وتوضحه جاز كأن يقال البليد: حيوان ناهق يدخل الحمام. (2) (3)
Fourth: It is not valid for the definition to contain a metaphorical term, unless it is accompanied by a clear and evident contextual clue (qarīna). The clue serves to clarify the intended meaning, even if it is metaphorical relative to the original usage; so the metaphor with a clue is actually a real meaning in its context. If we define the scholar, for example, as 'a sea that enters the bathhouse', or 'a sea that prays and fasts', this is not valid because it contains no evident clue that manifests the meaning, so the meaning is ambiguous. But if the metaphor is accompanied by a clue that explains and clarifies it, it is permissible, such as saying the dull-witted (al-balīd) is 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse.' (2) (3)
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth condition: avoid metaphorical terms without a clear qarīna (contextual clue). The author gives examples: defining a scholar as 'a sea that enters the bathhouse' is invalid because the meaning is ambiguous. However, if a clarifying clue is added, like in defining a dull-witted person as 'a braying animal that enters the bathhouse', the clue 'braying' specifies the intended meaning, making it valid. The footnotes (not included) discuss further refinements.

Condition Five: No Circularity (Dawr)
States the fifth condition: definition must not involve circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular definitions and explain why they are invalid.

Core Idea
A definition cannot rely on the very thing it defines.

The fifth condition of definition is that it must not be circular. A definition that uses a term that itself is defined by the definiendum fails to clarify and is logically invalid. The classical example is defining 'knowledge' as 'perception of the known' – but 'known' derives from 'knowledge', so the definition presupposes what it tries to define.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. In the example, it is 'ilm (knowledge).
2
Identify the definiens
Find the terms used to define it. Here, 'perception of the known' (ma'rifat al-ma'lum).
3
Check for derivational dependence
See if any term in the definiens is derived from or logically depends on the definiendum. 'Ma'lum' is derived from 'ilm.
4
Assess circularity
If a definiens term depends on the definiendum, the definition is circular and invalid because it fails to provide independent clarification.
5
Generalize the rule
Any definition where a part of the definiens is only known through the definiendum is circular and must be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامساً: لا يجوز أن يدخل في التعريف ما لا يعرف إلا بنفس التعريف فهذا يؤدي إلى الدور، فيمتنع. ومثاله تعريف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم، مع أن المعلوم تتوقف معرفته على معرفة العلم لاشتقاقه منه. ويمكن أن يجاب ببعض الأجوبة عن ذلك، ولكنها غير متبادرة إلى الذهن، وغير ظاهرة، والتعريف يجب أن يكون أوضح وأظهر من المعرف. (4)
Fifth: It is not permissible to include in the definition something that is only known through the definition itself, for this leads to circularity (dawr) and is thus forbidden. An example is defining knowledge as 'the perception of the known', while the known's perception depends on knowledge because of its derivation from it. Some answers can be given to this, but they are not readily apparent nor manifest, and the definition must be more obvious and apparent than the definiendum. (4)
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage explains the fifth condition: no circularity (dawr). The author gives the example of defining 'knowledge' as 'perception of the known', which is circular because 'known' derives from 'knowledge'. The footnote (not fully reproduced) mentions al-Baqillani's definition and possible responses, but the author notes these responses are not salient. The condition ensures the definition is independent and clarifies the definiendum.

Key Terms

معلوم

A) Because 'known' is derived from 'knowledge'
B) Because 'perception' is undefined
C) Because it is too vague
D) Because it uses a metaphor

Key Takeaway
The fifth condition of definition requires that the definiens not include terms whose understanding depends on the definiendum; otherwise, the definition is circular and invalid.

The Sixth Condition: Avoiding Ambiguous Terms Without Context
States the sixth condition: avoid ambiguous (mushtarak) terms without a clarifying context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a homonymous term is used in a definition without a clarifying context and determine whether the definition is valid.

Core Idea
A definition must not use a homonym unless context clarifies which meaning is intended.

In defining terms, clarity is paramount. One major obstacle is the use of mushtarak — words with multiple distinct meanings. If you define something using such a word without a qarīna (contextual clue), you increase obscurity rather than removing it. This lesson examines the sixth condition and its nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Read the passage: it states that using a homonymous term without a clarifying context is not permissible in a definition. Note the reason: it increases obscurity.
2
Analyze the first example
Consider 'the sun is an eye'. The word 'eye' is mushtarak, having multiple meanings (the sun itself, a spring, the organ of sight). Without context, the definition is unclear.
This example illustrates the violation.
3
Understand the exception
The author mentions that if all meanings of a homonym are intended, it may be permissible, but there is disagreement. Note that even then, some obscurity remains.
This nuance is important for advanced use.
4
Examine the second example
The definition of a proposition (qadiyya) as 'a statement that can be true or false' uses 'statement' (qawl), which is homonymous between spoken utterance and mental content. Here, both meanings are intended, so it is valid.
This shows the condition satisfied by intending all meanings.
5
Apply the principle
When crafting or evaluating a definition, check if any term is homonymous. If so, ensure a qarīna specifies the intended meaning, or that all meanings are intended and the definition remains clear.
This principle safeguards definitional clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً: لا يجوز استعمال لفظ مشترك بلا قرينة تعين المعنى المراد منه في التعريف، لأنه يزيد التعريف خفاء. وذلك نحو تعريف الشمس بأنها عين. وربما يجوز في حال إرادة جميع المعاني فيه، ولكن يبقى فيه خفاء للخلاف في صحة استعمال اللفظ في معانيه المشتركة دفعة واحدة، وذلك كتعريف القضية بأنها قول يحتمل الصدق والكذب، مع أن القول مشترك بين الملفوظ والمعقول، لكن لما أريد كل منهما صح. والأصل التنبيه لذلك كما أشرنا. (5)
Sixth: It is not permissible to use a homonymous term without a contextual clue that specifies the intended meaning in the definition, because it increases the definition's obscurity. An example is defining the sun as 'an eye'. Perhaps it is permissible in the case of intending all its meanings, but there remains obscurity due to disagreement on the validity of using the term for all its homonymous meanings at once. An example is defining a proposition (qadiyya) as 'a statement that can be true or false', while 'statement' is homonymous between spoken and mental, but when both are intended it is valid. The principle is to alert to that, as we indicated. (5)
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage explains the sixth condition: avoid homonyms (mushtarak) without a clarifying qarīna. The example 'the sun is an eye' is ambiguous because 'eye' has multiple meanings. However, if all meanings are intended, it may be acceptable, but there is disagreement. The author gives the example of defining 'proposition' as 'a statement that can be true or false', where 'statement' (qawl) is homonymous between spoken and mental. Intending both meanings is valid. The footnote (5) elaborates on the principle of alerting to this.

Key Terms
مشترك (mushtarak): homonymous; a word with multiple distinct meanings.
قرينة (qarīna): contextual clue that specifies which meaning of a homonym is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is defining the sun as 'an eye' problematic?
A) The sun is not an eye
B) 'Eye' is homonymous and without context, its meaning is unclear
C) It is circular
D) It is too general

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must either use unambiguous terms or provide a clarifying context for any homonymous term used.

Identify whether the following definition violates the sixth condition: 'Wine is defined as an intoxicating liquor.' (Assume 'liquor' is not ambiguous in this context.)
?
Consider the definition: 'Knowledge is a property that necessitates distinction.' Is 'property' ambiguous? If so, what qarīna would be needed?

Condition Seven: Excluding Accidental Properties from Definitions
States the seventh condition: definitions should not include non-essential properties (ahkām).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify accidental properties (ahkām) in a candidate definition and reject it if it includes such non-essential attributes.

Core Idea
Accidental properties describe, but do not define, essences.

A proper definition captures the essence of a thing through its essential attributes (genus and differentia). But it is easy to mistakenly include an accidental property—something that necessarily follows from the essence but is not part of it. This lesson shows you how to recognize and exclude such accidents, using the classic example of 'human' and 'laughter'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the condition
The seventh condition forbids including 'ahkām' (accidental properties) in definitions. These are properties that necessarily accompany the essence (lāzima 'an al-māhiyya) but are not constitutive of it.
In this logical context, 'ahkām' does not refer to legal rulings but to non-essential attributes.
2
Distinguish essential from accidental
Essential attributes (dhātiyyāt) are those that make up the essence, such as genus and differentia. Accidental attributes (ʿaraḍiyyāt) are external to the essence, even if they always accompany it.
For example, 'animal' is genus for human; 'rational' is differentia. 'Laughter' follows from rationality but is not part of the definition of human.
3
Examine the given example
The passage says defining 'human' as 'the laugher' (al-ḍāḥik) is invalid because 'laugher' alone is an accidental property. However, 'laughing animal' (ḥayawān ḍāḥik) is valid because 'laughing' functions as a differentia in combination with the genus.
The key is that when 'laugher' stands alone, it is a mere accident; when paired with a genus, it serves as a distinguishing feature (fasl).
4
Identify the genus and differentia in the valid definition
In 'laughing animal', the genus is 'animal' and the differentia is 'laughing'. The differentia must be an essential attribute that distinguishes the species within the genus.
Some logicians argue that 'laughing' is itself an accident of human, but the passage accepts it as a valid differentia for illustration.
5
Apply the condition to new definitions
When encountering a definition, ask: Does it include any term that is not part of the essence but merely a necessary concomitant? If so, the definition violates the seventh condition and must be revised.
Practice by checking definitions from Islamic sciences, such as those for 'worship' or 'knowledge'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعاً: لا إدخال الأحكام في الحدود، والمقصود بالأحكام هي الصفات اللازمة عن الماهية. فإذا أخذ الحكم جزءاً في التعريف لم يكن تعريفاً، بل هو حكم على الماهية، كما قالوا: لا يجوز أن يقال في حد الإنسان: هو الضاحك، بل يقال: هو حيوان ضاحك. (6)
Seventh: Do not include rulings (ahkām) in definitions. What is meant by 'rulings' are the properties that necessarily follow from the essence. So if a ruling is taken as part of the definition, it is not a definition, but rather a judgment upon the essence. As they said: It is not permissible to say in the definition of human: 'he is the laugher', but rather one says: 'he is a laughing animal'.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage explains the seventh condition: definitions (ḥudūd) must be composed of essential attributes (dhātiyyāt), not accidental properties (ahkām). The author clarifies that 'ahkām' here means properties that necessarily follow the essence (lāzima 'an al-māhiyya), such as laughter for humans. These are not part of the essence; including them would make the definition a mere judgment. The example: 'human is the laugher' is invalid because laughter is an accident; the proper definition is 'laughing animal', where 'laughing' is a differentia (fasl) that distinguishes humans, but it is not a mere accident if considered as part of a full definition. However, the point is that a proper definition (ḥadd tāmm) includes genus and differentia, not accidental properties. The footnote (6) further discusses that ahkām are not part of the essence and thus cannot replace essential components.

Key Terms
أحكام
حدود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Laughter is not unique to humans
B) Laughter is an accidental property, not part of the essence

D) It uses a homonym

Key Takeaway
A definition must consist of essential attributes (genus and differentia); including accidental properties turns it into a mere judgment, not a true definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check the following definition of 'wudū'' (ablution): 'Wudū' is washing the face and hands, and wiping the head and feet with the intention of removing ritual impurity.' Does this definition include any accidental property? Explain using the seventh condition.
?

Rule 7: Avoiding Circularity by Excluding Extrinsic Properties
Author prohibits including accidental attributes (ahkam) in hadd definitions, citing circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a proposed definition includes an extrinsic property (hukm) and explain why that leads to circularity (dawr).

Core Idea
A definition must capture essence, not consequences; including a hukm creates a vicious circle.

In classical Islamic logic, defining a term requires careful selection of essential attributes. This lesson explores the seventh rule: do not include ahkam (extrinsic properties) in definitions. The author warns that such inclusion leads to circularity, using a grammar example to illustrate the point.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between essential (dhati) and extrinsic (hukm) attributes.
The passage defines ahkam as attributes that are necessary consequences of the essence, not part of essence itself.
In logic, hukm here means a property like 'marfu' for fa'il.
2
Examine the example: defining the active participle (fa'il) as 'the noun in the nominative case (marfu)'.
The nominative case is a ruling that is not part of the essence of fa'il; it is a consequence of being fa'il.
Identify the hukm in the definition.
3
Trace the circularity.
To know that something is marfu, you must already know what fa'il is, because only fa'il nouns are marfu. So the definition presupposes knowledge of fa'il, making it circular.
Circularity (dawr) occurs when each of the two things depends on the other.
4
Apply the rule to any definition.
Always check if any part of the definition is a hukm that depends on the definiendum. If so, the definition is invalid due to dawr.
This rule is applicable across all Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعاً: لا إدخال الأحكام في الحدود، والمقصود بالأحكام هي الصفات اللازمة عن الماهية. كقولك في تعريف الفاعل: هو الاسم المرفوع، فالرفع حكم من أحكام الفاعل، والحكم على الشيء متوقف على تصوره فإذا أخذ الحكم جزءاً في التعريف توقف المعرَّف عليه وحصل الدور، الذي هو توقف كل من الشيئين على الآخر.
Seventh: Do not include rulings (ahkām) in definitions (ḥudūd). By rulings, what is meant are the attributes that are necessary consequences of the quiddity (māhīya). For example, your statement in defining the active participle (fāʿil): 'It is the noun that is in the nominative case (marfūʿ).' So, being in the nominative case is a ruling of the active participle. And the ruling about something is dependent on its conception. So if the ruling is taken as a part of the definition, the definition (al-muʿarraf) becomes dependent on it, and circularity (dawr) occurs, which is the dependence of each of the two things on the other.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage explains the seventh rule: prohibition of including extrinsic properties (ahkām) in definitions. The term ahkām here refers to attributes that are necessary consequences of the essence (māhīya). The author gives the example of defining the active participle (fāʿil) in Arabic grammar as 'the noun that is in the nominative case (marfūʿ)'. The nominative case is a ruling (hukm) of the active participle, not part of its essence. Including this ruling in the definition leads to circularity: to understand the definition, one must first understand the ruling, but understanding the ruling presupposes understanding the defined term. This circularity (dawr) makes the definition invalid. The passage emphasizes that definitions should only include essential characteristics (dhātiyyāt), not accidental attributes.

Key Terms
حكم
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, why is defining the active participle as 'the noun that is in the nominative case' problematic?
A) Because the active participle is not always a noun.
B) Because the nominative case is a ruling (hukm) that depends on knowing the active participle, causing circularity.
C) Because the definition is incomplete.
D) Because it uses an ambiguous word.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must consist of essential attributes only; including accidental properties (ahkam) leads to circularity (dawr), making the definition invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition: 'The subject (mubtada) in Arabic is the noun that is in the nominative case (marfu)'. Identify the hukm and explain why this definition is problematic according to Rule 7.
?
Propose a better definition for the active participle (fāʿil) that does not include any hukm. Base your definition on essential characteristics only.
